0345440900|4.0|I am surprised by the level of negativity in some of these reviews, and by the fact that some people really took offense to suggestions in this book.  Although not everything in this book worked for us, (as not every book is tailored just for one person!) I took from the book some info that helped me listen to my child and ultimately made both of us much happier.  For instance, at 3 months of age my son wouldn't nap and wouldn't nurse without falling asleep every time.  Therefore I was feeding him almost every hour and feedings were backing up into each other because he kept falling asleep.  After reading this and a few other books on breastfeeding, I attempted to implement our version of EASY.  It took about 3-4 days in which I worked very hard to give my child some version of a nap (standing over the cradle patting, picking him up and walking, rocking, putting him back down when he stopped crying, etc.)  At the end of the week he would take a good 30-45 minute nap every 2-2.5 hours or so.  This is a shorter time frame than EASY suggests, but it meant that he was rested enough when he awoke that he could nurse effectively.  He's 7 months old now and we still have a version of this routine in place with a longer EASY span and longer naps.  It really helped me understand that not every cry is a hunger cry, if you have some predictability in your day then you can diagnose that cry pretty quickly and give your child what he needs.

B0000DJZA5|5.0|I submit  the real reason McCartney in particular spearheaded this release of Let It Be...Naked was the rarely discussed second disc entitled Fly On The Wall. &quot;Fly&quot; reveals for the first time the real dynamics behind The Beatles break up. Simply put, McCartney's talents were still blossoming and growing while at this stage Lennon and Harrison were not in step with keeping up with  McCartney's single minded devotion to the craft necessary to keep this legendary band in working order.<BR>Here are a number of shockers which are revealed historically for the first time by &quot;Fly&quot;: <BR>You hear somebody playing a piano to the tune of Lennon's legendary song &quot;Imagine.&quot; <BR>At best, this new revelation means the tune can no longer be classified as strictly a Lennon solo creation. (At worst, how do we know it is not McCartney actually playing this melody?) It has been common Beatle knowledge that another standout on Lennon's solo career Child of Nature was a Beatle work in progress (appearing as &quot;Jealous Guy&quot;) and Harrison's seminal &quot;All Things Must Pass&quot; was another. It's almost as if Lennon and Harrison's greatest solo achievement were seeded as Beatles presumably with McCartney contributions sprinkled.<BR>McCartney directly addresses the other Beatles for bemoaning that &quot;things have gone downhill&quot; since the death of their manager Brian Epstein (who interestingly enough they all refer to as &quot;Mr.&quot; Epstein). <BR>As Paul says almost verbatim, it's as if daddy's gone now and that is the natural order of the world and it is time for Mr. Epstein's boys to stand on their own two feet; which they should be perfectly capable of doing having been fathered well for their formative years.<BR>Lennon expresses a concern most Beatle fans have been grappling with: How could a band that stands for &quot;all you need is love&quot; fall apart due to acrimony and disputes? <BR>It's always been a historical contradiction thatthroughout Lennon's campaign for peace, and Harrison's quest for global love that they were unable to coexist with each other or ever reunite. Fly reveals that the acrimony was inevitable due to the divergence of work ethic and appreciation for the significance The Beatles as between Paul on the one hand and George and John on the other.<br />Returning to the disparity of desire to strive for excellence between Paul and the other three consider the following examples sprinkled throughout Fly:<BR>Harrison good naturedly chides McCartney saying &quot;There he goes, playing the bass again&quot; to which McCartney responds by ripping into an amazing bass solo which manages to mimic (with Harrison's narration) an oboe and trombone. The disc then segues into a solo piano piece written and played by McCartney (never before heard by Beatle fans) that stands on a par with a J.S. Bach composition. This juxtaposed by sloppy playing on Get Back by both Lennon and Harrison on the song &quot;Get Back&quot; which has McCartney obviously frustrated and frighteningly aware these other three member were no longer on par with him.<BR>Even Ringo is cited for not putting effort into writing more after Harrison proclaims that a favorite song of Bob Dylan and The Band's was &quot;Don't Pass Me By.&quot; Harrison in general is infatuated with a childish grass is always greener view of the scene he recently returned from at Woodstock where in his words the guys all live together in a house..and happen to also be a band - making for a seamless work and living arrangement.<BR>But by far, the most enlightening nuggets of Fly comes in the various plans The Beatles have to cap off the movie documentary with a dramatic live show of some kind. At one point Lennon and McCartney are excited about the idea of performing on an ocean liner like the QE2. Harrison derides the idea as &quot;insane&quot; and too expensive. With historical hindsight can you Harrison's short sidedness in thinking a Beatle'sproject losing money in the long run?<BR>This desire to do something in terms of performing as long as it was unique and unexpected puts into a new historical light McCartney's rather bizarre early Wings tours where the band would get on a bus and showed up unannounced at a college campus. McCartney even through out the idea that the performance should end with the police and authorities showing up to halt the performance. The question now is was the rooftop raid by The British Bobbies a prophetic vision that McCartney had, or was the bust &quot;staged?&quot; <br />Notwithstanding all the light shone on The Beatles at this snapshot in time, Paul ends the disc in an amazingly humble and deferential fashion. He asks somebody &quot;Which way did John go?&quot; Meaning, in the end, Paul recognized Lennon for who he was and remained willing to follow his friends lead until it was no longer possible.

He was a simple man, who loved melodies, valued his privacy over his celebrity and always lent a helping hand to those less fortunate.  I highly recommend this DVD to one and all.

B0000DJZA5|5.0|[...] For me, reviewing this CD is tough, as Let It Be has always been my least favourite of the post-Pepper albums.  Some great individual tracks, but never a standout for me as a collection the way Abbey Road was and is.  That being said, the least brilliant Beatles album is still better than 99% of the pop music in existence.  In comparison to the original, this disc sounds fantastic to me.  How much of it is due to the stripping of Phil Spector's work, and how much of it is due to the wonders of digital remastering is tough to say.  Most of the tracks sound pretty much like the originals, until you play them back to back.  Hiss and distortion on the original CD version of this album that you never knew was there suddenly becomes very apparent when compared to the new versions presented here.  The instruments on the original version sound like they are being played through wet towels, and the vocals being pumped through a tin can by comparison.  Crisp, clean, and clear is the new.  Worth the price of admission all by itself.  Like most fans, I've invested many hundreds of dollars into my Beatles collection on CD, and if all of them were to be given the same treatment as this one from a clean up point of view, I'd go out and buy them all again.  As for the tracks with major changes, Across the Universe benefited the most in my opinion.  As indicated by a previous reviewer, The Long and Winding Road seems to have less of a \\"grand\\" feel, and preferences on that are going to be pretty much up to personal taste.  As with the other tracks, the vocals are much more clear than the Spector versions.  Anyway, there is much to like about this version of the album, if not for the major changes to certain tracks, for the wonderful clarity brought to others.  This is as close as you're going to get to having the boys playing live in your living-room.  All that being said, this doesn't change it's place in my ranking of Beatles' albums, and I suspect that will be the case for most fans.

0451199871|5.0|As a teenager, I devoured one Miss Marple mystery after the other, feeling all the time slmildly uneasy for wasting my time on such fluff. But as I grow older and read more and better, and as I'm less influenced by the opinion of the powers that be, Agatha Christie grows in my mind.<br /><br />Miss Marple is one of the most popular literary figures of the 20th Century, and yet she does not have the literary status of Sherlock Holmes. She's not required reading in high school literature courses, and she should be.<br /><br />Agatha Christie created an entire litererary genre, which is not the same as the genre created by Conan Doyle (whose unabridged Sherlock Holmes I'ver read with great pleasure cover to cover three times). I think it's fair to say that in some significant way, her mysteries are more similar to the novels of Jane Austen  than to those of Conan Doyle. Austen and Christie are fundamentally all about the motivations in human psychology. Where Holmes looks at a person's fingernails and deduces she is a musician, Christie watches a couple quarrel, and deduces they are really married, though they pretend otherwise. For her, such evidence is as solid as a footprint, and based on it, conclusions can be drawn.<br /><br />Nevertheless, she does revert to simple forensic evidence before convicting anyone. Her plots are always complicated, but Christie always provides a unique solution. If she did not, she would not be great. In th case of the Body in the Library,  there is as usual all sorts of evidence floating around to confuse you and occupy your mind, but 3 pieces of evidence in the end identify the murderer uniquely.<br /><br />An inattentive reader might finish the book concluding that had Christie modified the plot just a little, the outcome might have been different. Not so. Not any more than Dorothy could have stayed in Oz or Hamlet survived. Christie creates an entire self-consistent and carefully planned microcosm of motivations. However, in this case, the identities are veiled, the dynamo which drives the plot is the who-done-it. In this regard, her genre, of course, is in Doyle's tradition.<br /><br />And I so much like Miss Marple as a literary figure -- the enlightened being, British style. She lives in British society with its commoners and gentry, but is somehow neither. She easily consorts and sympathizes with both. She seeks neither wealth nor fame. She is content, fearless. Her mind and emotions are steady without being cold. And she does not pass judgement on her fellow man, though she stands unflinching in the face of justice -- Nemesis, she is called in one novel. She always knows who did it at the precise moment when all the evidence is in. In this case, that happened when the little boy showed her the clipped nails. Inspector Slack dismisses him as irrelevant. Marple does not. To her all men are created equal. Yet each is unique and understood on his or her own terms. Best of all, Christie accomplishes this through a truly endearing personality -- one with a penchant for tea and herbaceous borders, and a tendency, like most of us, to slip a stitch, though most of hers are literal rather than metaphorical

Well, I could be, but I don't want to jack the thermostat up to 80 degrees, so...  However, this version of the Fabs' swansong, which has had a can of Spector-Off sprayed on it, is justifiably worth the purchase, as it is quite revelatory on certain tracks.  The only song excluded here is the short ditty &quot;Maggie Mae.&quot;  Instead, we get &quot;Don't Let Me Down,&quot; which was recorded in the same sessions as &quot;Dig A Pony&quot; and &quot;I've Got A Feeling&quot; on 22 January 1969.<br />The &quot;Get Back&quot; here is a single version, with Billy Preston on keyboards.  &quot;Dig A Pony,&quot; sung by John, is more or less the studio version, and is a throwback to their rock roots.<br />The blues &quot;For You Blue&quot; with some different asides from George, though the part where he comments on John's slide guitar playing to Elmore James is intact.<br />And now, we finally get to here Sir Paul's immortal &quot;uncluttered piano ballad.&quot;  There's also some guitar, and organ that have been excavated beneath Spector's wall of sound on &quot;The Long And Winding Road.&quot;  This throat-lump eliciting song will still leave me in pieces, and is even more stripped down compared to the version Sir Paul did for Give My Regards To Broadstreet.<br />No silly voices for John on this rendition of &quot;Two Of Us,&quot; as it starts from the opening guitars.<br />The bluesy rocker featuring Paul and John singing together is left untouched, but boy, can Paul still rip it out Little Richard-style when he yells those &quot;yeah!&quot;  Dual themes can be seen here, from McCartney's title melody meshed along with John's &quot;everybody had a hard year&quot; motif.  Oh, and the clowny &quot;Oh Danny Boy&quot; sung by John is deleted from here.<br />The upbeat &quot;One After 909&quot; is a throwback to their early influences, but that's because it's a remake of a song they recorded back in March 1963 but didn't release on album.<br />&quot;Don't Let Me Down&quot; sounds different here, with a different ending and a more live feel to it.<br />&quot;I Me Mine&quot; is stripped of the Spector touch and it's gratifying to hear guitars, as is &quot;Across The Universe,&quot; which spawned two different versions of the single, one being the Spector version, the other being the World Wildlife fund remix found on Past Masters 2.  It's definitely quicker than the Spector, and just hearing the guitar makes quite a difference.<br />Then the tear-inducing title track, where one can finally hear organs, and none of the tape echo of Ringo's high-hat.  As for the guitar solo, it's not as hard-driving as the one on Let It Be, but not as muted as the single version.<br />The bonus disc is a 21 minute plus collection of studio chatter and brief song samples during the Beatles rehearsal in January 1969, which shows the Fabs acting cordial.  The song snippets include early fare such as the unreleased &quot;Because I Know You Love Me So&quot;, Ringo's &quot;Take A Trip To Carolina,&quot; and even twenty-one seconds of &quot;All Things Must Pass&quot;!  And the booklet contains excerpts from the Let It Be book, mainly dialogue between the Beatles.<br />Apart from hearing Let It Be the way Sir Paul wanted it to be heard, the other novelty is upon hearing them remixed and mastered in the current sound technology and not in the old AAD/ADD/DDD format.<br />As for Sir Paul's argument that this version is better, I agree that the versions of &quot;Let It Be&quot; and &quot;The Long And Winding Road&quot; are just as great, but me, I can live with both the Spector album and this one, which is quite a revelation in itself. 0345457684|3.0|As a fan of both hard boiled crime stories and cyberpunk scifi, this book sounded like it would be my ideal.  Unfortunately, it mixes those genres but does not succeed in them.<br />The book's biggest failure is the lack of common sense applied to the premise.  In this world, any mind can be put in any body, and in fact the protagonist was born Japanese but inhabits a hispanic body throughout the book.  This concept is throught provoking but I felt it was mishandled by Morgan.  For instance, prison terms are served by putting a person's mind in storage while the body is given to someone else to use.  Since the mind has no consciousness, this amounts to punishment by time travel.  Also, characters constantly flip flop as to whether they associate their identity with their bodies or not.  At one point someone will say &quot;all bodies are the same&quot; and at another he will lament not having his original body.  This cognitive dissonance could have been an interesting concept to explore but it seem the book doesn't take the idea too seriously.<br />The writing is also workmanlike.  Morgan indulges himself is boring, cliched dream sequences throughout the book.  His attempts to imitate the classic hard boiled allegories manage to seem out of place primarily because they occur too infrequently.  That being said, the pacing is quite good and the book is a page turner.  Thought the story was ultimately overblown, I did manage to care enough to finish the book.<br />I think Morgan shows promise.  For his next book, he could use a better editor to reel in some of his sillier ideas.  But I will keep an eye on his work in the future.

B0000DD7LB|4.0|Britney Spears was always great to look at and her performances were so extravagant, you couldn't help but look (even though they're lip synched).  On her first three albums, the music was just okay.  The same bubble-gum production ruled throughout those records, despite trying to make it sound different.  Now, with In The Zone, Britney has decided to ditch the producers of the past and buff up her sound.  The result is a groovy and deep resonance that makes you marvel at just how good Britney can sound.  She never had a great voice, but this record uses it in the best possible context and it sounds wonderful.  <br />The 12 songs (and one remix) on this record have the privilege of sounding different from one another.  By now you've all heard the first singls, \\"Me Against The Music\\" featuring the diva Britney aspires to be, Madonna.  But that track is far from representative of the rest of the album.  The sultry \\"Breathe On Me\\", which might be a single, is simply brilliant.  It sounds slightly reminiscent of Kylie Minogue ... .  \\"Early Mornin'\\" is another great track which should be a single.  This one is produced by Moby (and it's obvious).  Also check out the controversial \\"Touch Of My Hand\\", the sweet \\"Everytime\\", the erratic \\"Toxic\\", and the guilty-pleasure Hilary Duff-like \\"Brave New Girl\\".  This album unfortunately features the worst Britney song ever, the second single and track, \\"(I Got That) Boom Boom\\" featuring the Yin Yang Twins.  The guest artists sound so horrible it makes you wince, which is a shame since Brit's part is actually kinda catchy.  <br />But aside from that song, this is a very good record and a breakthrough for Britney herself. B0000DJZA5|3.0|It's got to be said, so I guess I'd better be the one. While the Naked version of Long And Winding Road most definitely destroys the overblown Montiovani-like production on the original release, other songs like Across The Universe, I Me Mine and even Let It Be suffer. Spector's version of Across The Universe wisely slowed down the tempo, the tape speed reduction making John's voice sound more Beatle-y and added droning tambouras and tasteful strings. On the song Let It Be, Naked goes for the muted guitar solo used on the single whereas Spector picked the superior fuzztone drenched solo, its nasty tone deflating the pomposity of the tune itself. And it should be noted that the horns &amp; strings on this number were arranged by George Martin not Spector. Plus, the new version eliminates a crucial drum overdub in the final verse. The Naked version of I Me Mine uses Spector's trick of repeating a verse to extend the song's length while omitting Spector's tasty string overdub. Overall, the new mixes sound a bit shrill by comparison to the earlier ones. A lot of the personality the original album had has been destroyed by eliminating the between song chatter. And I don't care what you say, the song Get Back is supposed to end with John saying &quot;I hoped we passed the audition.&quot; Maybe it wasn't always so, but after 30 years of hearing it that way it's become a crucial part of the song. So while it's nice to have this (after all, I did buy the thing), overall I still like the earlier version better.

This Lord of the Rings film trilogy will be embraced by audiences for years to come.<br />But is all this praise good enough for Jackson?  No.  Jackson wanted to go the extra mile and present to us fans the second part of a trilogy, the movie that he knew we wanted to see in theaters.  And he presents it to us in this Extended Edition of The Two Towers.  This movie is not just 43 minutes longer, it is 43 minutes BETTER!  The only way you will ever see a movie like this in a movie theater is on Trilogy Tuesday, prior to The Return of The King, on December 17, 2003.<br />What will you get in this Extended Edition?  A lot.  I mean, A LOT!  I do not want to spoil it for any of you, because it is best that you just stick the DVDs into your DVD player, push Play, and just watch the movie to see what a difference 43 minutes makes.  But a couple of scenes I will mention include Eowyn asking Aragorn on how old he is (87, BTW) and a homage to Tom Bombadil (yes, finally, for those of you thought that Tom Bombadil was permanently left out of The Lord of the Rings, Jackson has listened and responded!).  Oh, and Miranda Otto showcases her singing during a scene where they bury King Theoden's son.<br />This Extended Edition of The Two Towers also requires a superior sound system.  The movie has been remastered in DTS 6.1, which is superior digital surround sound.  You will notice the difference.  When Saruman announces that &quot;A new power is rising&quot; to 10,000 Orcs, the chants are loud and furious!!!!!  Imagine beingin a football stadium of about 80,000 screaming fans.  That is how loud the chants are.  And the Battle of Helm's Deep...run for cover, it is an earthquake.<br />I have yet to browse through discs 3 and 4, but I can say that Peter Jackson has clearly developed a tremendous adaptation of one of the greatest pieces of literature ever written.  The Extended Editions of both The Fellowship of the Ring and The Two Towers prove it.  Now we have to wait another year for the Extended Edition of The Return of the King!

B0000E1ALR|5.0|Creating a multi-disc greatest hits set on Bruce Springsteen, even within the context of Sony's acclaimed \\"Essential\\" series, was bound to present problems. His acclaimed 30-year career gathered fans from disparate camps. He had those remembering the huge impact of 1973-75's opening albums and repeat 70s concert legends. He had Top 40 fans joining for 1984's multi-platinum \\"Born in The USA\\" but left as his style turned away from that dated LP's anthems (Springsteen even joked of being a teen idol at 35 on 1995 greatest hits CD). Finally, he has hardcore fans seeking everything released yet seeking out bootleggers (against Springsteen's wishes) for more.<br />You can argue with this 3CD set's sequencing and song selection. \\"My Hometown\\" should have been here; it captured the heartbreak of Springsteen's beloved Freehold and Asbury Park slipping away while, even more than the now-cliched \\"Born in the USA,\\" allowing him the wide screen shot of 1984 America. Chronological sequencing deprives the listener of hearing how Springsteen wrote on similar themes (unemployment, indifference, loneliness), widening and deepening his characters' vision while songs grew more sparse. (Clarence Clemons, still Springsteen's onstage foil, is heard only in the margins after songs from 1980's \\"The River&quot;). It makes Disc Two a harder, more somber listen. But these 47 songs successfully show Springsteen's greatest gift to rock: his ability through sheer musical will to raise mundane human interaction to high art and anthemic drama.<br />It came down to ambition and adulation of his rock and roll idols. Bruce Springsteen wanted to tell Bob Dylan's stories with Van Morrison's spiritually soulful voice (\\"Rosalita,\\" \\"Blinded By The Light&quot;). He wanted to sing like Roy Orbison atop Phil Spector production (\\"Born To Run,\\" \\"Hungry Heart\\"). He wanted a band who could compress every 60s 45 into one performance, at once if needed, and wanted to front that band as James Dean, Marlon Brando, and Elvis Presley (sharing those legends' association with transportation as empancipation). He found it in his E Street Band, creating songs as grand and melodramatic, even as bloated and cloying (Springsteen always loved the huge count offs), as those classic songs were.<br />The songs sound better than ever thanks to Bob Ludwig's remastering, probably presaging re-release of Springsteen's catalogue in Sony's SACD format.  The extra songs are worthwhile for diehards (\\"From Small Things\\" waters down Chuck Berry, \\"County Fair\\" tenderly evokes, memories of a summer carnival, Jimmy Cliff's \\"Trapped\\" a dramatic performance originally released on 1985's \\"We Are the World\\" LP.) But it's Springsteen's collected works, intelligently gathered and presented, which makes \\"Essential Bruce Springsteen\\" a sterling introduction and near summation of this American rock and roll icon.nd Elvis Presley (sharing those legends' association with transportation as empancipation). 0679428895|4.0|Everybody thinks they know about the eruption of Mt. Vesuvius at Pompeii--79 AD . . .rivers of bubbling magma . . .citizens immortalized in pleading poses for all eternity by ash . . .the heedless rich getting their comeuppance from nature. Those basics are true, but Robert Harris reminds us that the eruption of Vesuvius was much more than that. It remains one of history's greatest and most dramatic disaster stories, and we know a great deal about it because one of the Roman Empire's greatest historians was there to write a blow-by-blow record of the destruction; and although Pliny did not survive, his report did.    Pompeii and Herculaneum were the Malibu and Santa Barbara of Rome. In the hot August of 79 AD, tourists were swarming to the cool coast to enjoy the luxury accommodations, crystal swimming pools, and elegant spas of the bayside resorts. Marcus Attilius is there too, but  he's not there to enjoy the occasional cool breeze, he's there to work as the new aquarius of the Aqua Augusta--the new water engineer in charge of the enormous aqueduct that brings endless water flowing to the nine towns around the Bay of Naples. Springs are failing for the first time in centuries and the flow of water is being disrupted to hundreds of thousands of people. Attilius' family has worked on the great aqueducts for generations, but even he is bewildered by the cause of this crisis somewhere along the Aqua Augusta's sixty-mile line--a line that stretches along the slopes of Mt. Vesuvius.    The Roman aqueducts were an amazing feat, and Harris describes their workings in great detail. He does an excellent job of showing, not telling, and through Attilius and his crew he weaves an incredible amount of information into the narrative and it is fascinating. Also excellently done is his description of the various effects of the eruption--which lasted several days--where he uses Pliny's observation of the event of excellent effect. Pliny, historian and general, was also a very fatand cranky old man by 79 AD. He took one of his ships out into the bay to watch and record the devastation from what he thought was a safe distance. But too soon the ships in the bay were in danger from the roiling waves and huge chunks of pumice flying down off the mountain. Pliny had his scribes don helmets and take down his descriptions as clods of pumice bounced off the old general's uncovered head--\\"The pumice is less like rock than airy fragments of a frozen cloud.\\" he dictates. \\"It floats on the surface of the sea like lumps of ice. Extraordinary!\\" Eventually it would clog the bay and begin to crush ships. Pliny knew he was too heavy and unsteady to escape the final firestorm from Vesuvius and ordered his scribes to save themselves and his precious reportage. Fortunately they did, and Robert Harris puts Pliny's observation to fine use in this novel.    Harris is a workmanlike writer with the gift of being able to integrate complicated information into a believable narrative. That's what made \\"Enigma\\" and \\"Fatherland\\" so interesting, and what works for \\"Pompeii.\\" The characters are take second place to the setting, and are not particularly exciting. However, they respond to the extraordinary circumstances around them in ways that are completely consistent with their characterizations. It is the same with Harris' establishment of place. He offers no special explanations of Rome, but builds it all into the action. As a result the Roman world seems very immediate and almost modern.

The style is horrible to begin with and then, when you slap a cut-rate singer on it, it just flounders. The aforementioned &quot;Everytime&quot; would be a great ballad if it had been sung with a little more conviction or, better yet, by someone who could actually sing.<br />One of the biggest problems with this disc (when it's not the hip-hop tracks) is the fact that the arrangements and production is so spare that it leaves Britney's vocal out to fend for itself. If that's not cruelty, I don't know what is! Britney just doesn't have what it takes to be a good sing. At least she doesn't anymore. She's gotten lazy. If you see footage of her as a young teen, her voice has a lot of depth and strenth. Now she growls a wind-up to each phrase and coos out the remainder of it. It's barely a wheeze at times. The poor thing can't even be bothered to sing live when she performs. I and many others find themselves increasingly ripped-off by this tactic. She really isn't growing as an artist. She's just changing the pieces of her wardrobe, musical and otherwise.<br />I hope that Britney continues to evolve and take risks. This was a noble attempt but her lack of vocal talent holds her back. Ifshe were to, say, take a cue from her partner in crime on &quot;Me Against the Music&quot; and get some vocal lessons in to help her out (and possibly save her voice from early burn-out) she may save herself from a backward slide into vapidity.

B00005JL9B|5.0|One thing to be said about Japanese horror/extreme action is that it could seldom, if ever, be accused of being formulaic.  Some of the most ingenious storylines ever thought of have come from Japanese films(see Ringu, Suicide Club, Kairo, Uzumaki).  But while the ideas are in place the problem is often with the execution of the film.  Overacting, cheap special effects and slow pacing between action scenes have often plagued the Japanese films I have bore witness to.  Not so with Battle Royale.  The concept is pure genius and believe me, the execution even better.<br />Kitano is a strict teacher whose source of pride comes from being able to instill discipline in the young.  He is for the most part very successful at this and highly respected among his students.  Then one afternoon, an out-of-control student who he reprimands stabs him in the back of the leg.  Following this incident Kitano quits his teaching job and disappears out of sight.  Fast forward to a few years later.  A group of 42 students(who appear to be in their mid-teens) board a bus to go on a class trip.  At some point during the bus ride a sleeping gas is unleashed and all the students fall asleep.  When they wake up, they find themselves cramped in a classroom and with a tight metallic necklace tied around their necks.  To their great surprise, in through the door walks Kitano, who most have had as a 7th grade teacher.  Kitano informs them of a game he has devised called Battle Royale that they have been randomly selected to be a part of.  Battle Royale is Kitano's sadistic vengeful way of getting back to the youths he feels lack discipline and `mock' adults.<br />The rules behind Battle Royale are this; The students are now on a remote island where all 42 of them will be pitted against each other in a game to the death.  Each student is given a backpack that includes water, food, a weapon, a compass and a map of the island.  The last person standing gets to go home free.  There is a catch however.  They only have 3 days.  If more than one person is left standing after 3 days, their collars will all blow.  The island is also mapped with regions that contain shifting `danger zones', of which Kitano informs them of over a loud speaker.  If someone is still standing in a danger zone after Kitano makes the announcement their collar will explode.<br />From the moment the rules of Battle Royale are transmitted to the kids, the film's frenetic pace never lets up.  Director Kinji Fukasaku pulls absolutely no punches here, making this a no-holds-barred extreme version of Lord of the Flies that is compulsively watchable.  The acting performances he is able to draw out of these kids is remarkable.  Part of what Fukasaku seems to be trying to achieve with this film is to give us a character study of how different some kids can be from others and how differently they can react when faced with peril.  One group commits suicide instantly, refusing to be a part of the sadistic game they're forced to play.  Another group spends their time shacked up in a cabin, trying to figure out a way to hack through Kitano's system so they can get the collars off their necks.  One group of women simply decide to live as normally as they usually would by cooking, exchanging stories, staying civilized.  And of course, some kids choose to become killing machines, determined to be the one to get out of the game alive.<br />Director Fukasaku's choice of filming locations is absolutely stunning, from the lush tropical jungles, to the edge of the sea, the lighthouse, etc.  The film's image looks surprisingly sharp and clear for a non-hollywood production.  The only thing I would have to complain about are the English subtitles.  They are filled with grammatical errors and poor sentence structuring.  I also got the impression that many parts of the dialogue were missing or were not presented accurately.  Hopefully, Battle Royale will see a North American DVD reissue that will be dubbed or at least have better subtitles.  I'm not hoping for a North American remake however.  It would probably be a PG-13 `message' film starring the likes of Frankie Muniz and the Olsen twins. Ugh!!<br />Battle Royale is an action classic not to be missed.  It's adrenaline-fuelled pace will appeal to action fans, it's bloody carnage and gore will appeal to horror fans.  This is a film that puts just about any North American action film to shame. A modern cult classic that deserves a wider distribution.t least have better subtitles.  I'm not hoping for a North American remake however.  It would probably be a PG-13 `message' film starring the likes of Frankie Muniz and the Olsen twins. Ugh!!<br />Battle Royale is an action classic not to be missed.  It's adrenaline-fuelled pace will appeal to action fans, it's bloody carnage and gore will appeal to horror fans.  This is a film that puts just about any North American action film to shame. A modern cult classic that deserves a wider distribution.

B00004ZBVG|4.0|High octane action and the perseverance of some of the leads elevate \\"Bat-21\\" to that higher level of Vietnam movie.  Basically the story of how a high-level intelligence officer eludes capture after being shot down behind enemy lines in Vietnam, \\"Bat-21\\" is relentless and never exploitative.  Hackman is Iceal Hambleton, a USAF colonel who fights the war in Vietnam from the offices and golf courses of Saigon.  Tasked to identify communist missile sites, he boldly (and unwisely) tags along on an EB-66, an unarmed electronic warfare bird loaded with intelligence equipment.  The sole survivor after being shot down, Hambleton finds himself pursued by the enemy who apparently appreciate his value as a prisoner.  While the enemy (was it the NVA or the Cong? I could never tell) is unable to find Hambleton, they prove more than capable of making life hell for the rescue choppers sent to rescue him - including the crew of a \\"Jolly Green\\" cut to pieces over an enemy-held village.  While on the ground, Hambleton stays in contact with Bart Clark (Danny Glover), a USAF captain who flies spotter missions for more heavily armed bombers.  (Clark flies FAC - from his slow-moving Cessna, he finds targets for the strike fighters, marking them with white-phosphorous rockets).  Knowing that the enemy is monitoring them, Hambleton and Clark work out a code that posits the map of Vietnam as a big golf course.  As rescue attempts become more dangerous, Clark finds himself drawn ever deeper into the rescue, climaxing in his going rogue when his superiors throw in the towel.<br />\\"Bat-21\\" isn't really a classic.  There's plenty of thrilling action, and the script even manages to find room for irony (as when Clark pauses during a radio conversation with Hambleton while he zaps a few communist troops).  Still, nothing really convincingly explains how Hackman manages to elude capture...by anybody (the script makes clear that he is definitely no Rambo).  The film also breezes over the biggest irony - that Hackman's code is based on his smarts on the golf course rather than the battlefield.  If the film excels over cheap action flicks, it's in the determination and exhaustion of its leads and persistent refusal to avoid Hollywood glory.  Also, unless anybody knows of another film, this is the only one I've seen that captures the critical role played by Forward Air Controllers in both prosecuting the air war in Vietnam, and rescuing those airmen nearly lost.

B00008RH1H|5.0|Of all Shakespeare's great tragedies, Macbeth is the play that people find the most troubling. It is the one work in Shakespeare's canon that comes closest to Greek tragedy with its outward inevitability and its conclusions seemingly predestined by the fates. We are also asked to identify with a murderer--an intelligent intriguing complex murderer to be sure--but still a murderer. This creates within us a moral tension not present in more clear-cut tragic figures like Hamlet and Lear, and this only leads to a greater incentive to excuse Macbeth as a victim of fate. It is easier to identify with a murderer who has no choice in his actions since his lack of choice absolves us of the guilt we might otherwise feel for sympathizing with him.<br />Kurosawa leaves us no such outlet. His Macbeth is an unrepentant schemer, opportunist and murderer. That he feels passing pangs of conscience is due to the remnants of his humanity, remnants that by the end have been totally subsumed by his overarching ambition. This is a subtly different presentation from the angst ridden second guessing Macbeths so often portrayed in western productions, and when Kurosawa's Macbeth meets his death, we are struck not so much by the waste of a potentially good man as we are by how completely he is destroyed through the workings of divine justice.<br />I am not going out of my way to be bookish here. This analysis is meant to show how well Kurosawa understands the moral message conveyed by the play as he constructs a Macbeth who, in spite of the ghastliness of his character, both fascinates us and draws our admiration. It is easy to admire obvious heroes and just as easy to condemn obvious villains and Hollywood has made a fortune from grinding away at this formulaic mill over the years. However, we are cast into contrary waters when we find ourselves admiring sympathetic villains and condemning failed heroes. Such characters are too much like our secret selves and their presence forces us into uncomfortable self-examination. This is Kurosawa's purpose: he is forcing each of us to ask how much like Macbeth we really are. How much blood would we spill for our own chance at a throne?<br />And Kurosawa does this under an all but impossible handicap. He cannot use Shakespeare's glorious language, so in its stead, he substitutes his genius in the filmmaker's craft: mood, lighting, shading, contrast; and most of all, imagery, both the shrewd and the shocking, like the famous closing scene in which justice is delivered with altogether gruesome finality. Nor does it hurt that he has access to one of the finest actors in cinema. Toshiro Mifune puts in an inspired performance as an unswervingly driven Macbeth.<br />This film and 'Ran', Kurosawa's later Shakespearean adaptation, are both bittersweet experiences. Kurosawa never had the chance to direct a production in the Bard's native tongue. He lived and filmed in a time when it was unthinkable for a major western production to be given into the hands of a Japanese director, so although we have had many notable interpretations of Shakespeare from many notable directors over the years, the now lost prospect of a definitive version from one of the best directors in history can only evoke regret.<br />This film is unusual in that it may appeal even to people who do not like Shakespeare. In sacrificing the language for his Japanese audience, Kurosawa is forced to compensate with his theatrical ingenuity, and this has the ironic effect of making his version more accessible to those who have always been put off Shakespeare because of the demands of the language. Against this, the acting is comprised of bold gestures and pregnant pauses, a style that may strike some as stilted and affected, but which actually comes from traditional Japanese Noh theatre and which grows on you and becomes more natural as the film progresses.<br />This is not a film for mass audiences, but if you like intelligent atypical challenging fare andare not afraid of subtitles, then this surprisingly original adaptation will make you think as well as enthral and entertain you.

B00005JKN9|3.0|A troubling movement persists in art these days that invests brutality and depravity with a patina of panache. Apparently, if perversion is presented with enough flair, the professional critics and the viewing public can be conned into thinking it edifying.<br />This is how sociopaths, murderers, and parasites achieve glory. In an older simpler age, they were dismissed as the scum of society. In our more sophisticated one, they are elevated into New Age Philosophers, spouting pearls of &quot;street&quot; wisdom, feral insight and anti-heroism. Where we once honoured &quot;life, liberty and the pursuit of happiness&quot;, it is now &quot;death, nihilism and the dregs of despair&quot;.<br />This movie is about death, nihilism and the dregs of despair. As such, it is sadly appropriate to our brutish times. The characters are psychopathic prima divas addicted to the sound of their own voices even more than they are to killing. There is such a profusion of soliloquies, oratory and high-flown rhetoric that Scorcese is clearly playing to Shakespeare. He manages to achieve a pale success: imitating the Bard's method while failing his larger purpose.<br />This is a revenge play. A son tries to avenge the death of his father, but must first overcome his own reluctance, a reticence that stems from self-doubt and concern about usurping the natural order. Sound familiar? It should. Shakespeare dealt with this thesis in one of his greatest plays. But unfortunately, this movie isn't Hamlet, hard as it may try.<br />And that is the essence of its failing. Hamlet's vengeance exists within a larger moral framework, and comes at a high price. &quot;'Vengeance is mine,' saith the Lord&quot;, and Hamlet pays for his presumption with his life, daring to appropriate to himself a justice reserved for the divine. But this film panders to vengeance. The anti-hero kills and then walks off into the sunset with the girl. In contrast to Hamlet, there is no moral cost. &quot;Go thou and do likewise&quot;, says the film, and in saying it, exposes the corruption that claims its soul.<br />There is a scene in this movie where the antagonist tells us how he lost his eye and both the protagonist and the audience are spellbound by the quality of the telling. Daniel Day Lewis embodies his character so consummately and Scorcese films the scene so masterfully that we watch mesmerized as this psychopath, with a lyricism verging on poetry, embarks on a long soliloquy describing his fight, his disgrace, his self-styled atonement and his eventual murder of the man who spared his life. It takes a steadfast moral sense to reject the depraved philosophy of a man whose twisted idea of honour necessitates the killing of the one who let him live. &quot;I buried him in his blood,&quot; says Bill the Butcher. &quot;Of all those I have killed, he was the only one worth remembering.&quot; Later, he is himself killed in a pool of his own blood, and only after he too first spares the life of his future killer. And of all those whom Amsterdam Vallon kills, we suspect that Bill the Butcher is also the only one worth remembering.<br />A marvellous device. The killer's code comes full circle. But there is something wrong here. Isn't this an affirmation of the Butcher's philosophy? Doesn't Vallon, in the act of killing the Butcher, validate what the Butcher stands for?<br />The power of this film comes from such technique: psychopaths are given heroic stature; anti-heroes are so anti-heroic that they are beyond redemption; lives come full circle, then spiral inward into destruction; the most accomplished of storytelling techniques are used in the service of the most deplorable of human specimens. It goes on. I am sure film buffs can cite dozens of perfectly executed samples of the filmmaker's craft: technique turned on the clever lathe of genius. But what is all of this genius dedicated to? The advancement of a philosophy expounding death, nihilism and despair.<br />The anti-hero shouldhave died in this film. That would have saved this story by giving it a moral heart and allow it to partake of tragedy. Like Hamlet, Amsterdam Vallon would have achieved a standing in death unattainable in life. He could have symbolized the understanding that vengeance exacts a price and leads to a cycle of death. Instead, we are given a moral perversion. Scorcese tells us that vengeance belongs not to the Lord, but to he who wields the longest knife. It's okay to kill, so long as the man I kill is worse than the man I am.<br />Judged by its technique, this film deserves five stars. Not many films achieve such mastery of method. But judged by its purpose, it is a failure. Speaking to nothing, it deserves nothing. Your enjoyment of this film will depend on how much you value its virtuosity over its degeneracy.

B00005JLEU|5.0|What a glorious, glorious labour of love. After watching it, I felt like hugging myself. Calling this film an 'animation' and thus lumping it together with the childish fantasies turned out by lesser talents doesn't do it justice, for this work of art deserves comparison to the best in any genre.<br />This is a film that has complex imperfect heroes, no villains, trials more internal than external, a world more vibrant than our own, a spirituality so deep as to reach the metaphysical, and yet will delight a girl of ten as much as a lifelong moviegoer of ninety. It is also surreal in tone, lyrical in composition, invokes tension and suspense without malevolence, and the filmmaker achieves all of this in an animated cartoon. `Spirited Away' is a work of sheer genius.<br />This film has pity and compassion, love and redemption, hidden strength and discovered courage, turbulence harnessed to an inner harmony; but it never strikes a false note nor succumbs to maudlin sentiment. Through gentle humour and understated dignity, it shows so much respect for the intelligence of its audience that I wanted to kiss the director's hand.<br />Calling this film a fairy tale just won't do. Unlike western animation, its magic is deep down, at the very roots of its make-believe world, so deep that it is worked into the very fabric of the story. There is no faerie flying around sprinkling pixie dust, no bubbling cauldrons, no evil-incarnate stepmother and no goodness-personified prince charming; nothing so obvious or so crass. Instead, the world of spirits into which our little heroine lands is as natural as breathing. In this world, humans are the intruders, and as gross material entities, it is we who bring disorder and upset to their realm.<br />It is not easy describing what makes this film so special, because the reasons range from the very small to the very large. At the small end, the film's eye for detail is simply marvellous. Even the act of a little girl putting on her shoes is given such character that we pause to admire the filmmakers' respect for children. At the large end, this film shares with us a journey of spiritual awakening. Our little heroine grows over the course of the film from a self-pitying self-absorbed little mouse into a loving openhearted daring soul.<br />But a word of caution: this is not standard cartoon fare. It assumes an intelligent audience and therefore dispenses with a paint-by-numbers plotline. Indeed, it isn't even about plot but about character, places, feelings, moods, and most of all, themes. For example, a recurring theme is that of purging. Various characters throw up throughout the film and this may disturb the fainthearted. But, if one is familiar with oriental thought, expelling poison to cleanse oneself is a common cultural theme and does not provoke the same disgust that it does in occidental cultures. Indeed, after our little heroine replenishes the river spirit, his gift to her is the medicine of purging. Later in the story, she saves a number of her friends by putting it to good use.<br />Obviously, this is a film that must be approached with some thought. Unlike most western animations, one cannot view it while idling in neutral. It has much that is beautiful and transcendent, but it also has terror and pain, and it demands our most careful consideration. Those unwilling to invest the required effort will get nothing out of it and had best stick with Bambi. Yet, for all its demands, this is a film that remains accessible even to children. Younger viewers may even have an advantage over their parents because they will approach it with open minds unconstrained by western styles and conventions. For them, the film's symbolism may not be apparent, but the filmmakers have applied such symbolism so gracefully that it will still be felt if not noticed, and this will be all that matters.<br />This film rewards open minds. To fully appreciate it, one must cast away ones preconceptions about animation.Western practice and tradition has sadly fenced animation into a marginal niche by considering it children's fare that is incapable of higher artistic expression. 'Spirited Away' is Hayao Miyazaki's exuberant response. It deserves the same consideration and accolades that we habitually heap on 'real' films and is frankly so far above the excrement endlessly churned out by Hollywood that it inhabits a different plane. This film deserved the Oscar in 2001, not for 'Best Animated Film', but for 'Best Picture'. It really is that good.

Especially in &quot;Let it Be&quot; and &quot;The Long and Winding Road,&quot; Preston's fantastic keyboarding is much more distinguisable than the original recordings. Actually, it's a weird 20-minte voice and song mosaic of the &quot;Let It Be&quot; sessions.  If it costs extra money to get this &quot;bonus&quot; disc, don't bother because it's dumb.<br />Anyways, the Beatles are the best group ever, so if you're a big fan, this album is a must.
B0000DJZA5|4.0|The release of &quot;Let It Be . . . Naked&quot; is sure to prove a long term dilemma for radio stations.  Do they play &quot;The Long and Winding Road&quot; with its orchestral overdubs added by &quot;Wall of Sound&quot; producer Phil Spector, or do they play the &quot;new,&quot; stripped down version that's been &quot;de-Spectorized?&quot;  For my money, I'll take the latter version for two reasons.  First, the Beatles had never intended the &quot;Let it Be&quot; sessions to be augmented by other musicians, save the addition of Billy Preston's keyboard contributions.  Second, the sound on this CD is, arguably, the best that I've ever heard on a Beatles release . . . it is simply a superb mix.<br />The &quot;Fly on the Wall&quot; bonus CD is somewhat of a letdown for me.  Checking in at just under 22 minutes, you hear snippets of the Beatles in conversation during the recording sessions as well as some brief musical excerpts.  Why couldn't this have been longer . . . particularly the musical pieces (the longest musical piece on this CD lasts about a minute and a half)?  Surely some alternate takes could have been included.  That said, the &quot;Fly on the Wall&quot; disc is still a must have for fans of Rock's greatest band . . . The Beatles.

B000002L82|5.0|Whenever someone complains that electronic music is totally void of warmth or realism, I just point to this album. New Order play electronic music with the urgent and manic shifts of rock. Bernards vocals are earnest yet detatched, with guitar work that is jagged, random and sparse; Gillian and Stephen's percussion and synth sequences are both lively and rigid, an up-beat/down-march; Peter's basslines are fluid yet kinetic. This is a work of ironic friction. The warmth and humanity flow thru the restrained and urgent detatchment. The whole album sounds like a friend that wants to say something but can't, hiding it behind his/her eyes.<br /> I would consider Power, Corruption &amp; Lies an artistic/pop masterpiece in the true sense. The electronic and post-punk meanderings are only the charms that envelope the wonderfully angular pop sense that Bernard brings to his lyrics. Everything is so vague and pretty; it's like the album cover...just a random slice of still-life, full of colour and restraint. Tracks like 'Your Silent Face' or '5-8-6' explode with edgy, manic shades of light, sorta like impressionism via expressionism.<br /> You won't be let down by this album. With the band themselves producing it, it's a natural workout of rock and electronics, perfectly blended together to make a classic.

B0000VCZK2|5.0|You (almost) can't ask for more! All four films of this landmark and trendsetting sci-fi/horror series, including theatrical and extended versions of each of these unique films plus countless hours of behind the scenes footage and stills creates what can only be described as one of the most, if not THE most, definitive DVD collection available!<br />Too bad, then, that there are glaring omissions in this otherwise superb offering. Why is it, for instance, that there are apparently no interviews, commentary or featurettes with Sigourney Weaver?? Without her, the series would be infinitely less accessible and interesting. Her role in \\"Alien\\" was a groundbreaking, gender-bending eye-opener, and her Oscar-nominated performance in \\"Aliens\\" was equally revolutionary.<br />Second, why was \\"The Alien Legacy\\" substituted for \\"The Alien Saga\\"? The latter, narrated by John Hurt of \\"Alien,\\" was a spectacular and riveting analysis of the making of each film in the series and their relevance. It provides a wonderful, insightful bookend for the series, and includes interviews with the principals, including Weaver!<br />Lastly, while some featurette material focuses on H.R. Giger, I wonder if it's too little? This master's imprint on the series - and subsequent sci-fi/fantasy films in general - is indelible and unmistakable. His creepy-yet-beautiful artistry not only created arguably the most memorable creature in film history, but supplied the wondrous alien environments as well. Directors come and go, but his influence was omnipresent throughout the series and should not be overlooked.<br />I mention these shortcomings simply because they're glaring in such a stupefyingly comprehensive collection. This is a must-own for me and should be for any serious fan of film. Twentieth Century Fox is to be commended for a collection that truly honors the series' fans and film connoisseurs in general.

B0000DJZA5|5.0|At first glance, it could be argued that &quot;Let It Be... Naked&quot; differs only slightly from the version we've been used to for the past thirty-three years, yet these differences--program sequence, the substitution of two alternate takes, the deletion of one track, and the removal of Phil Spector's infamous &quot;wall of sound&quot;--add up to an album that is substantially more cohesive than the original, and has a harder, more muscular feel. It seems inevitable that Beatles fans will now be divided into two camps--those who love this album, and those who hate it. And the point has been made that it is impossible to tell whether this version is, as the publicity claims, the album that the group originally &quot;intended.&quot; All that said, the fact remains that &quot;Let It Be&quot; is much improved in this new incarnation. In this case, the whole is indeed more than the sum of its parts, and these seeming subtleties give a controversial classic a long-overdue new lease on life.

B0000DJZA5|5.0|Just to clarify for anyone who doesn't know what the deal is with this album, a summary follows: The Beatles were working on a concert/film/album entitled &quot;Get Back&quot;, which was intended as a &quot;back to basics&quot; album.  During the sessions, the band started to break apart, but moved on and made their final album, &quot;Abbey Road&quot;.  While they worked on &quot;Abbey Road&quot;, &quot;Get Back&quot; (now retitled &quot;Let It Be&quot;) was sitting around, and they let others take care of it without much involvement.  Eventually John Lennon brought legendary producer Phil Spector in to add his &quot;wall of sound&quot; treatment, overdubbing strings, orchestras, etc., which seemed to Paul to be the opposite of the original intent of the album (which they had agreed would have no overdubs).  Partially because of this (and, in particular, the overdubbing of strings on &quot;The Long and Winding Road&quot;), the Beatles broke up prior to the release of &quot;Let It Be&quot;.  (Ironically, &quot;Abbey Road&quot; actually made it out before &quot;Let It Be&quot; did.)<br />The surviving Beatles, Paul and Ringo (and, briefly, George) were somewhat involved in this project, which they fully supported, to remove Phil Spector's influence on the album.  The two albums are amazingly similar and startingly different. &quot;Naked&quot; is a much cleaner disc in many ways, the most obvious being the remastering and the removal of Phil Spector's overdubs.  Even the new track list seems preferable for several reasons.  &quot;Dig It&quot; and &quot;Maggie Mae&quot; have been removed, and I don't think many will particularly miss either.  The far superior &quot;Don't Let Me Down&quot; has been added, which was previously availble on &quot;Past Masters, Volume II&quot; in a slightly different format.  The new sequencing often feels more natural for reasons  I can't quite put my finger on.  I rather enjoy the combination of &quot;Two of Us&quot; followed by &quot;I'veGot a Feeling&quot;, but this is nothing I couldn't accomplish on my MP3 jukebox.  The removal of Phil Spector's overdubs does vastly improve the album, &quot;The Long and Winding Road&quot; in particular.<br />It seems at first that &quot;Naked&quot; is the clearly superior version of the two.  However, the album has lost a bit of its charm by cleaning itself up a little too much.  For instance, the dialogue snippets between songs featured on the original &quot;Let It Be&quot; are nonexistant, including the classic line about &quot;I hope we passed the audition&quot; at the end of the album.  I feel the removal of this is something of a shame, but this is one more reason why it is interesting to compare the two albums...  In an odd bit of irony, I also wonder if this may have been the worst choice of a Beatles album as far as remastering goes.  Somehow the slightly grainy sound of the original seems more suited to the purposes of the album.<br />And coming back to those purposes, you will be disappointed if you buy this album expecting &quot;Get Back&quot;.  This is, as mentioned above, exactly what it claims to be in the title: &quot;Let It Be&quot; with overdubs removed.  Anyone expecting more may end up feeling that they have thrown their money away, especially if they already have &quot;Let It Be&quot; and &quot;Anthology 3&quot;.<br />Which version is better?  It's hard to say.  &quot;Let It Be... Naked&quot; is probably better as a Beatles studio album, but may not be what people expected.  It has an excellent song selection, and is worth many relistens.  It may not be worth it if you already have &quot;Let It Be&quot; and the stripped-down versions of the songs featured on &quot;Anthology 3&quot;, which contains a de-Spectorized version of &quot;The Long and Winding Road&quot;, among others.  You could basically put this album together from those two, so this is a bit disappointing.  The bonus disc (described below) does not contain any significant portionsof previously unreleased songs.  The liner notes are interesting, but not especially revealing for long-time fans.<br />With all of the little minor complaints, why rate it 5 stars?  Because it is truly a great album standing on its own, regardless of any specific expectations that anyone may have of it.  And despite having &quot;Let It Be&quot; and &quot;Anthology 3&quot;, I still enjoy having this alternate version of the album.  Definitely worth a look/listen, especially if you're not familiar with the original.<br />BONUS DISC:<BR>The bonus disc contains twenty minutes of material on a single track.  The songs are played in these jam sessions are listed below. All lengths are in seconds:<br />Sun King (0:17)<BR>Don't Let Me Down (0:35)<BR>One After 909 (0:09)<BR>Because I Know You Love Me So (1:32) (Previously unreleased)<BR>Don't Pass Me By (0:03)<BR>Taking a Trip to Carolina (0:19) (Previously unreleased)<BR>John's Piano Piece (0:18) (Previously unreleased)<BR>Child of Nature (0:24) (Previously unreleased)<BR>Back in the U.S.S.R. (0:09)<BR>Every Little Thing (0:09)<BR>Don't Let Me Down (1:01)<BR>All Things Must Pass (0:21)<BR>She Came in Through the Bathroom Window (0:05)<BR>Paul's Piano Piece (1:01) (Previously unreleased)<BR>Get Back (0:15)<BR>Two of Us (0:22)<BR>Maggie Mae (0:22)<BR>Fancy My Chances With You (0:27) (Previously unreleased)<BR>Can You Dig It? (0:31)<BR>Get Back (0:32)<BR>(The rest of the time is taken by dialogue.)<br />The disc is interesting, but not incredibly revealing.  You hear snippets of conversation that give you an idea where the album is going.  Many may not feel inclined to listen all of the way through, although I did enjoy the disc.  Since these were not intended to be actual recordings, they go in and out of each song, so even those listed as a minute or more are rarely continuous.  You can see a bit of solo material here, notably George Harrison's &quot;All Things Must Pass&quot; (later released on the album of the same title).  Perhaps most interesting is &quot;Child of Nature&quot;, which is brief, but very clearly the prototype of &quot;Jealous Guy&quot; from John Lennon's &quot;Imagine&quot;.  Interesting, but don't get the album just for this disc.

B000001DTM|5.0|This could very well be my favorite album of theirs. I got my first dose of U2's music during 1995 when I first heard \\"New Year's Day\\" and another song called \\"One\\" on the radio in Chicago around that time. I eventually discovered that these songs were by U2 and eventually discovered their other hit songs from their \\"War\\" album through \\"Achtung Baby\\" and I have been a fan ever since then. U2's 1991 magnum opus \\"Achtung Baby\\" is easily one of the greatest rock albums ever made. There are a lot of reasons why this album is so. The music on this album is like nothing that they've done before or since then and is one of the greatest examples of a band completely reinventing itself. It's so hard to believe that this album, being so far ahead of it's time, is actually 12 years old now. \\"Achtung Baby\\" is so innovative in such a manner that it does not sound the slightest bit dated. To tell you the truth, U2 never sounded better than on this album.<br />The politically charged anthems that defined their 1980s albums are laid to rest and are replaced with more introspective lyrical writing dealing with everyday life and relationships but from a rather gloomy point of view. Musically speaking, they went into a totally different arena, moving away from the atmospherics of their 80s works and incorporated some grunge rock combined with punching industrial techno dabbling on here and the result is phenomenal. Every song on here is absolute brilliance like nothing else on any rock album. \\"Zooropa\\" while great, sometimes lacked the humanity and warmth that this album has and focused more on simply the electronic techno elements. \\"Pop\\" fared better at this. \\"All That You Can't Leave Behind\\" kind of reminds me of this album to a degree but without a lot of the industrial electronica on here.<br />\\"One\\" is easily one of the greatest ballads U2 ever wrote and it's evident that Bono reached his peak on this song. This is a highly dramatic and emotional song that gradually builds up and reaches a climatic end. This album and song in particular was one of several songs on this album that got me through a rough point in my life after a close friend of mines died in a car accident in early 1999. As a result, \\"Achtung Baby\\" not only is one of two favorite albums by them but this album means something special to me. \\"Trying to Throw Your Arms around the World\\" is another track that helped me get through that sad period in my life and thus that song is a highly moving track. It's a very bittersweet and passionate song that gives me goose bumps every time I listen to it. Even before all the turmoil set in, these two songs made me a forever a fan of U2. The hit singles on here are just marvelous. \\"Even Better than the Real Thing\\" is just straight out fun. \\"Mysterious Ways\\" gets my nod as one of the best `sex appeal' songs that I've ever heard by any rock band or artist. The guitar punch at the beginning alone is unforgettable and the song has a highly danceable hip-hop oriented beat combined with electrifying guitars.<br />Even the non-singles on here merit a lot of attention. The opening track \\"Zoo Station\\" is the best opening song of any U2 album behind \\"A Sort of Homecoming\\". It starts with very odd guitar riffs and then electronic mechanical beats come in and the song becomes a very strange blend of rock, industrial and pop all mixed in one. Bono's distorted vocals on here are awesome to listen to and blend in perfectly with the songs mood instead of clashing with it. \\"Who's Gonna Ride Your Wild Horses\\" is in my opinion one of U2's most underrated classics of all time being a highly dramatic yet fun song. I can't believe that this song didn't make it on their \\"Best of 1990-2000\\" collection. Some seem to not care much for \\"So Cruel\\" which is a shame as it's a chilling yet highly intelligent classic with an eerie and slightly creepy vibe to it. I'll admit, it took much longer for it to grow on me than the other songs but I now finally find it to be a marvelous song. The final track \\"Love Is Blindness\\" is a dark and mournful song with a very bitter and cold atmosphere to it. It starts with a haunting organ-like intro and becomes a mid-tempo rock oriented ballad with a dark and moody tone to it. Upon feeling that love and relationships seams to be becoming too media-oriented, I can really relate to the song title at times. This is an excellent closer to this album. The rest of the songs I didn't mention with this review also deserve a listen but the songs I mentioned are among the best on the album.<br /> While \\"The Joshua Tree\\" may be their commercial peak, \\"Achtung Baby\\" is by far their greatest and most ambitious artistic achievement in just about every way. I love both \\"Zooropa\\", \\"Pop\\" and even enjoy \\"All That You Can't Leave Behind\\" a lot too but from my point of view, there is something that \\"Achtung Baby\\" has that seems to be missing on their latter works.. B00005V9HH|5.0|The Usual Suspects is a very effective movie for several reasons.  One lesser reason the film is good is the surprise ending, which is a shocker and comes at you from out of nowhere.  However, I don't think the movie depends completely on the ending; it works regardless.  In fact, I don't think the ending is all that great, for reasons I will mention later.<br />The story centers around a supposed drug bust gone bad.  Roughly 90 million dollars worth of narcotics were on a ship and a group of notorious criminals were assembled to get the money.  The very beginning of the film shows that things got out of hand and the boat actually exploded, with practically all people in the vicinity being killed.<br />The criminals were chosen by a character named Keyser Sose (so-say) who is so renowned for his criminal actions that the mere mention of his name brings panic and fear.  We see a flashback of his cold blooded nature: when thieves took over his house and threatened his family, he walked in calmly, shot his family first, and then shot the bad guys.  The implication is that he will kill instantly and without remorse.  He is almost mythical, no one has ever seen him and lived to tell it, and some don't even believe in his existence.  All five criminals (played by Gabriel Byrne, Kevin Pollack, Kevin Spacey, Benicio del Torro, and Stephen Baldwin) have done something to hurt Sose in the past, and so this is their chance to redeem themselves.  The cast is great, all bring the proper amount of intensity to their roles.<br />After the explosion, only Verbal (Spacey) is left.  He is brought in for questioning, given immunity for his information, and on his way out the door, answers a few more questions for Agent Kujay (Chazz Palminteri), where he talks about the whole situation.  We only see what happened through Verbal's eyes.  In this way, the movie is like the classic Rashomon or the more recent and much less effective Basic, where we see information through the eyes of onevery closely involved.  How much of what we see is the truth is the real mystery.<br />The ending is a surprise, and very well done, but I don't think the movie gave enough clues in order for us as viewers to figure it out.  In that sense, it almost cheats a little, because it's easy to fool someone when you don't give them a chance to solve the riddle.  Even keeping that in mind, though, this movie is one of the better mystery thrillers I have seen in some time.

B0000009TJ|5.0|I have to take issue with kireviewer's review.  This is a recording of the band, not just one element of it, but the entirety.  The song list is excellent and it is not just a pile of novelty tunes.  Unfortunately, one really needs to understand the historical perspective to be able to fully appreciate these recordings.  I recommend &quot;Electric don Quixote&quot; by Neil Slavin for those unfamiliar with the intricies of Frank's professional life.<br />As just one example of what I'm getting at, this disc was a change of style with respect to how Frank had been assembling his recorded music.  First off, many of his albums had a foundation from tracks recorded live, with some tracks pulled off of the multitrack live master, then overdubs (sometimes MANY overdubs) were later added in the studio.  At other times, solos were pulled off of one live tape and dubbed onto backing tracks from another performance - at times from a totally different song and maybe with the tape speed or even direction changed.  Frank was a tireless pioneer of studio technology and recording and mastering technique and what you heard on a disc often never happened anywhere but in his imagination, which was then crafted in the studio into the product.<br />This album was a huge switch, back to earlier days in a way.  These are live recordings with ZERO overdubs.  If you want to know what his music sounded like live back in the 80's, just listen to these discs.  His bands never quite met his expectations, but they were leaps and bounds, even light years ahead of any other band I've ever heard live - and I've heard quite a few that claimed to be the best.  Now, like I said, there are no overdubs, but what was done in some circumstances was to take part of a song from one performance and edit into a different performance, to capture the best parts of each.  It's easy to tell when this was done by reading the liner notes.  For example, &quot;The Eric Dolphy Memorial Barbecue&quot; was assembled from performances in Syracuse, NY and Detroit, MI, but don't expect to hear the edits.  I was at the Detroit show and that's my voice you hear in the background, saying: &quot;You guys are the greatest!&quot;  And they were.<br />About the song list: again, excellent.  19 tracks were from one location, 8 were assembled as mentioned above from two different locations.  Tunes from all periods of Frank's career, with numerous cover tunes thrown in for color, including Ring of Fire, I Left My Heart In San Francisco, Bolero, Purple Haze, Sunshine Of Your Love, When Irish Eyes Are Smiling, Godfather Theme Part II, Theme From Bonanza and Stairway To Heaven.  How's that for variety?  Of the Zappa tunes, we are treated to new arrangements of numerous tracks, including Heavy Duty Judy.  We have some excellent instrumentals, including Zoot Allures, Sofa #1 and an incredible performance of The Torture Never Stops.  Lots of American folks didn't get to see this tour, but I did and it was incredible, just like this set!!!m performances in Syracuse, NY and Detroit, MI, but don't expect to hear the edits.  I was at the Detroit show and that's my voice you hear in the background, saying: &quot;You guys are the greatest!&quot;  And they were.<br />About the song list: again, excellent.  19 tracks were from one location, 8 were assembled as mentioned above from two different locations. How's that for variety?  Of the Zappa tunes, we are treated to new arrangements of numerous tracks, including Heavy Duty Judy.  We have some excellent instrumentals, including Zoot Allures, Sofa #1 and an incredible performance of The Torture Never Stops.  Lots of American folks didn't get to see this tour, but I did and it was incredible, just like this set!!!

B000002KCC|5.0|Rarely can music that is this bad be so good. This is what happens when five or six guys decide to throw a wake for a couple of fallen comrades, lock themselves in a studio with a lot of booze and drugs and record the results. The playing, naturally by Young's grungy band Crazy Horse, is generally as woozy and out of tune as Young's singing. Yet the context of &quot;Tonight's The Night&quot; is the downside of fame, particularly an indictment of hard drugs, which permeates the majority of the songs. Particular gems are &quot;Speakin' Out&quot;, &quot;Tired Eyes,&quot; &quot;Albequrque&quot;, &quot;Lookout Joe&quot; and of course, the infamous title track, offered at both the start and closing of the album (the first, but not the last time Young has done this.) As a statement of freedom of expression, this album stands alongside &quot;John Lennon/Plastic Ono Band&quot; for all it's brutal rawness. This music is not'produced', in the normal sense of the term. You hear mike hits, mistakes (and the subsequent chuckles), voices that can't quite hit the notes (&quot;Mellow My Mind&quot; in particular)...I'm sure a lot of people's reactions was, &quot;Is this the same Neil Young who made 'Harvest'?!&quot; Yes it is. An album like &quot;Harvest&quot; is the work of a songwriter writing songs. &quot;Tonight's The Night&quot; is the work of a songwriter telling it like it is. Musical fame is not all ladies and limos; it can be dark and you can die from it. This may be a depressing message, sure, but you have to admire Young and his musician's for not caring and just letting it all hang out.

B000002UD0|4.0|Shunning the political sloganeering of his previous solo albums, on &quot;Walls And Bridges&quot; Lennon set out to make &quot;Plastic Ono Band&quot; Part II. Here we have Lennon once again exploring his inner feelings, with the perspective of somebody who has sobered up after a long binge, which was indeed the case when the album was recorded. Separated from Yoko Ono, Lennon had spent a year in Los Angeles drinking, smoking and snorting himself to death while making a failed attempt at getting back to his roots. Then he came to his senses, which is where &quot;Walls And Bridges&quot; comes in. Easily the most produced (posibly even overproduced) of all of his solo work, most of the music borders on adult contemporary rather than the raw rock and roll Lennon based his reputation on. He discusses his newfound sobriety (&quot;Whatever Gets You Thru The Night&quot;, a duet with Elton John which became an unlikely #1, &quot;Nobody Loves You When You're Down And Out&quot;), loss of Ono (&quot;Bless You&quot;, &quot;Going Down On Love&quot;) and even business manager Allen Klein's betrayal (&quot;Steel And Glass&quot;, which attacks Klein in the same way &quot;How Do You Sleep?&quot; did to McCartney.) Of course, there is some old Beatles-style surrealism (&quot;Old Dirt Road&quot;, &quot;#9 Dream&quot;) and even a guitar and horn laden instrumental (&quot;Beef Jerky&quot;) just to ease the melancholy feel of the rest of the material. Yet, after the failure of &quot;Sometime In New York City&quot; (an album doomed to failure no matter what) and &quot;Mind Games&quot;, you sense that Lennon was conciously trying to recapture the Beatles style in the arrangements. Perhaps trying too hard...in my opinion, the five &quot;Walls And Bridges&quot; demos found on the posthumous &quot;Menlove Avenue&quot; album are superior, since they are stripped down ala &quot;Plastic Ono Band&quot; and fit into that context better (apparently the demos for all the &quot;Walls And Bridges&quot;tracks can be found on a bootleg called &quot;Something Special And Rare&quot;.) In hindsight, considering &quot;Double Fantasy&quot;'s weaknesses: the collaboration with Ono, and Lennon's determination to fall back on cliches, &quot;Walls And Bridges&quot; is his most varied album stylistically and his last real solo album. But you couldn't blame him for 'retiring' afterwards...he'd pretty much said and done it all.

As a Christmas album 4 stars, as a Tull album 3 on the understand that a 3-star Tull disc is better than most all the drivel on the radio...since it's for the holiday season it gets a seasonally appropriate, well-earned 4 stars.  If it weren't a Christmas album, it would still be a welcome addition to the catalog, but as a Christmas album it's cozy and familiar, which is what we want at Christmas.  Happy holidays.
