A truly headphonic experience, the highest highs (&quot;Let Down,&quot; &quot;Subterranean Homesick Alien&quot;) of this album will make your heart and soul soar in epileptic, robotic happiness. Conversely, the lowest lows (&quot;Climbing Up the Walls,&quot; &quot;Lucky&quot;) will make your skin shrivel up in goose-pimpled fright. Meanwhile, the stale apathy of songs like &quot;No Surprises&quot; will freeze your heart to the core and make you cry tears of utter sadness and loss. On the very first listen, &quot;Paranoid Android&quot; will give you the strange yet undeniable impulse to simultaneously weep uncontrollably and violently smash everything in your household. The computerized &quot;boops&quot; that precede that manic war of a song will become your most favorite random noise of all-time, guaranteed. Furthermore, the subtle, quiet beauty of &quot;Airbag&quot; and &quot;The Tourist&quot; will, at the beginning and end of the album, respectively, provide both the question and the inevitable answer that lies at the heart of this incredible concept album. Never before has an album essentially about unfeeling cyborgs emitted so much raw emotion and humanity. A true masterpiece.

0780020685|5.0|If the title seems to be hyperbole, I should point out that it's not just my opinion, but the opinion of almost all movie critics.  This movie is on many top-ten-movies-of-all-time lists, for good reason.  For many years, this movie was seen only in a shortened form, but Criterion has restored it to its original cut as first released.  Not only that, but it has included a superb commentary that was so good that after I watched the DVD for the first time without commentary, I started to watch it with the commentary thinking I would spend a few minutes and was so fascinated that I watched the whole movie again just to find out what the commentator had to say!  And this is a long movie, over 3 hours long, but it doesn't drag.  <br />The basic story (ripped off by the inferior U.S. remake, The Magnificent Seven) is pretty straightforward.  Bandits attack a village and take its crops and some of its women.  When the villagers learn that the bandits plan to return, the decide to hire itinerant samurai to defend it, with the only pay being room and board.  They find a remarkable samurai as the leader, and he recruits the other six, including an expert swordsman who lives for his art (the actor who played this part had never handled a sword before this movie, and never did learn to ride a horse!), and a crude bumbler (played by star Toshiro Mifune)  who is actually a farmer's son pretending to be a samurai.  Incidentally, Mifune was originally considered for the part of the expert swordsman, a role which he played brilliantly in two other Kurosawa movies, Yojimbo - the basis for Clint Eastwood's hit A Fistful of Dollars, and Sanjuro.  But this is no simple good versus evil story.  Even though the villagers have hired the samurai, they don't trust these &quot;heroes&quot;, and hide all their women.  And it appears that in the past the villagers may have killed other samurai and hidden their armor - when the samurai discover this, they are not sure they can trust the villagers either.  This ambiguity adds depth to the story.  All this is gradually revealed as the remainder of the film shows the samurai training the villagers, attacking the bandits to cut down their numbers, and finally, after a few skirmishes, having the climactic battle scene in the driving rain and mud, which captures the chaos of battle as well as anything ever done in the movies.  Unlike most movies where the battle scenes always seem to be staged, with the big explosion center screen and nothing much else going on, Kurosawa seems to embed you right in the action so you feel as if it is going on, not just in front of you, but all around you, off- screen as well as on. This is great movie-making, and the commentary explains how he achieved this effect.  And at the end, the villagers go back to rice planting, and the samurai &quot;heroes&quot; stand to one side - no triumphal banquet and procession for them.  Now that their job is finished, they are ignored.<br />As I mentioned, even though the film is long, it doesn't drag, because Kurosawa omits unneccessary exposition.  One example, early on after one of the samurai is killed in battle, we have a burial scene.  Mifune's character, in grief, grabs a battle flag made by the dead samurai, runs on top of one of the huts in the village and defiantly plants it on top of the roof. The camera then pans up to the hills beyond the village, where we see the bandits descending to attack the village.  In a few seconds the mood changes from grief to exhilaration as the long awaited battle is joined.  <br />On the other hand, Kurosawa also includes images which, although not strictly necessary to the main narrative line, will remain with you long after the movie is over.  An example, at one point the samurai locate the bandit's hideout, and set fire to it to force the bandits out.  As the samurai look into the hideout, they see a woman, captured by the bandits.  In a silent scene, except for a lone flute playing plaintively on the soundtrack, this woman awakens, sees the fire off-screen, starts to cry out, then decides to remain silent, even though it means she will die a horrible death.  The usual martial arts movie would never even consider having a scene like this, but this short, wordless scene speaks eloquently about this nameless woman's condition and deepens the story.  It is little touches like this that linger.<br />Martial arts movie with incredible battle scenes, character study, social commentary, as with all great art, there are a lot of different ways you can view this movie.  At the time he made it, Kurosawa said he wanted to revitalize the samurai sword movie, which he considered a dead form.  He did more than that - he transformed it!  A lot more could, and has, been said about this movie, but bottom line:  Nobody who loves movies should miss this - it is essential!

The Screen Behind the Mirror is the conclusion to the first half of Enigma's music.  It's still as wonderful as the other cds, just something different.

B0000DJZA5|5.0|Finally, after more than 33 years, the non orchestral arrangement of Let it Be has been released! On Nov 14, I purchased highly advertised Let it Be...Naked 2-CD set here in Japan. There was a long line to get the new set, so I knew it must be good. I must say, I was quite impressed with the sound quality, and the removal of Phil Spector's various orchestral and choir arrangements.<br />Now for the comparisons of the two albums. First of all, as many people expected, the sound quality is much better, and there is virtually none of the original tape hiss (The only exception is Disk 2, which is mostly studio chatter)<br />Now for the songs:<br />\\"Get Back\\" suffers from being cut by some 38 seconds. The Naked version of Get back is only 2:34, compared to the 3:12 original release. The song is exactly the same otherwise.<br />\\"Dig a Pony\\" is also the same, but it does not have the false start that was included on the original.<br />\\"For You Blue\\" seems to be unchanged, just much crisper, without any hiss.<br />\\"The Long and Winding Road\\" is a totally different take. The Naked version is slightly faster and without the orchestra. Some of the words have changed. The part that goes \\"Anyway, you'll never know, the many ways I've tried\\" has been changed to \\"Anyway, you've always known, the many ways I've tried. Another change in lyrics is where \\"Don't keep me waiting here\\" becomes \\"Don't keep me standing here\\" The end portion where we hear Paul say \\"Yeah, yeah, yeah, yeah\\" is not on this version. This is my favorite song on the new release.<br />\\"Two of Us\\" the song starts immediately, without the short intro that is on the original. The song itself is the same.<br />\\"I've Got a Feeling\\" sounds the same, but Paul and Johns voices have been put on the center channel now, which provides more range for the instruments.<br />\\"One After 909\\" does not sound any different.<br />\\"Don't Let Me Down\\" is a song which was not included in the original Let it Be Album from 1970. It can be found on Part Masters II. This version is different from that version. This version has not just Lennon, but the other Beatles singing as well.<br />\\"I Me Mine\\" is nearly the same. The only difference I noticed was that the stereo separation was slightly modified.<br />\\"Across the Universe\\" is now a beautiful Acoustic song, without the orchestra or choir. This is one of the best songs on the Naked release.<br />\\"Let it Be\\" is a different take. A little shorter, by maybe about 15 seconds. Paul's voice has more life to it. The organ is much more defined and louder. The backing voices of the other Beatles sound much better. The guitar solo is different, but better I think. The drums toward the end are somewhat different, but only serious Beatle fans will notice it. There is none of the orchestral arrangement from the original. This is a great song, and I think this new version is better.<br />The 2nd disk, called \\"Fly on the Wall\\" has one track, about 22 minutes in length consisting of various Twickenham sessions from 1969. This disk is a treat as it shows the Beatles attitiude in the studio where they recorded their music. I am assuming this starts Jan 1, 1969 due to everyone saying \\"Happy New Year\\" to each other. There are a few minutes of various Beatle songs such as \\"Don't Let Me Down\\" and \\"One After 909\\" It is quite humorous because you hear the Beatles talking about the song \\"One After 909\\" they talk about how they never really thought about the meaning of the lyrics in the song.<br />I don't want to spoil it, but this disk is quite great: both humorous and serious. If you listen well, you will even hear Yoko Ono laughing and speaking in the background at one moment. These various sessions show how the Beatles had arguments such as Ringo Starr's decision \\"Not to go abroad\\", and how they should all split up to do separate projects.<br />To sum it all up, this new release is exceptionally good. The best songs on the album are \\"The Long and Winding Road\\", \\"Let it Be\\", and \\"Across the Universe\\", All of the songs sound great in their new Digitally Re-Mastered and \\"naked\\" form. I hope this set receives as much success as the Beatles \\"1\\" did.<br />Enjoy the version of \\"Let it Be\\" the way it was always meant to be heard!heir new Digitally Re-Mastered and \\"naked\\" form. B0000CD5I6|5.0|This CD shines as brilliantly as the Christmas star itself. Mandolins, flutes, whistles, drums, guitars, accordian, organ, and sweet glorious vocal harmonies -- they're all tucked perfectly together in this simply amazing holiday CD. In my estimation, this one is destined to be Rock's flagship contribution to the holiday genre. Compare to other lagging efforts by Tull's contemporary peers, for example, the Moody Blues and Chicago, and you'll see what I mean. Tull's is at once both happily optimistic and seriously reflective, both musically and lyrically, whereas the others, although not bad, just don't fire on all cylinders as does this one. And it's appeal is intergenerational, judging by the fact that both my children and my elderly parents enjoy it as well. As is typical with Jethro Tull, it is difficult to pigeon-hole this CD. It's not Rock, it's not Jazz, it's  not Celtic, it's not Classical, it's not World, and it's not Folk, yet it integrates elements of each into some fantastic greater whole. It's simply amazing -- a real holiday masterpiece from a venerable, veteran band obviously at the peak of its game. Do yourself a holiday favor and buy this CD -- for yourself, your family, and your friends, neighbors, and colleagues -- just share the experience. It's just too good not to!

B000002KDO|5.0|While \\"Paranoid\\" had the great individual songs (War Pigs, Paranoid, Iron Man) it was really on \\"Masters Of Reality\\" that Black Sabbath really came together left their mark upon music. This is without a doubt Black Sabbath's darkest record, a cohesive slice of dark rock that would create heavy metal.     Seemingly coated with layers of tar, Iommi's guitar riffs are the centre piece of this black hole of a record. Everything else seemingly hides behind the sludgy riffs, even Ozzy's wail seems to come from behind the heavy chords. Even the folky (though almost gothic) \\"Embryo\\" and \\"Orchid\\" the accoustic interludes somehow fuse perfectly into the records flow, escalating the sense of creepy darkness.     Surprisingly the 'softer' song \\"Solitude\\" is one of the best. Managing to escape the self indulgence that many heavy metal bands sink into why they attempt to show their 'tender' side. \\"Children Of The Grave\\" meanwhile seems to gallop towards the apocalypse the band seem to fear is imminent.     More over the flow of this album mimics the stages of depression leading to suicide. \\"Sweet Leaf\\" and \\"After Forever\\" signal escapism and a search for redemption, \\"Embryo\\" as well as \\"Orchid\\" are musical equivalants of inertia. The rest is fear of impending doom (Children Of The Grave), cynicism (Lord Of This World), despair (Solitude) and finally the anger and frustration that lead to a suicidal act in \\"Into The Void\\".     And still people claim heavy metal has no soul. Compared to \\"Masters Of Reality\\"'s endlessly black one, everything else seems shallow.      Sabbath would never make an album this great again, though they still had quite a few great songs in them. Ozzy may have became a public joke and Iommi may have driven the band towards medicore oblivion, but with albums like this and Paranoid to their name their reputations as musical icons is secure.

B0000DJZA5|5.0|Admittedly, the title of this release is a drag, as is the cover art.  From what I understand, \\"Let It Be...Naked\\" is not what the band had originally intended as the \\"Get Back\\" album (which was never released, the material for which was rehashed by Spector and eventually released as \\"Let It Be\\"), but certainly \\"Get Back\\" would have been a better title than \\"Naked\\".  And who knows what other kinds of original cover art the art directors at EMI could have devised, but it most likely would have been better than what is on this release.  Having said that, this release is a triumph musically.  I doubt you will find many people making the claim that this music, either in this release or in the original Spectorized version, is The Beatles' strongest material; \\"Abbey Road\\" makes for a better swan song for the band, and earlier albums such as \\"Rubber Soul\\" and \\"Revolver\\" show the band at their peak. But the music here is still of a definite period in The Beatles' career, and is as essential to any Beatles collection as any other release.  The original \\"Let It Be\\" obscured the sound the band was attempting to achieve during this period, and while the album is not awful, it certainly is not The Beatles getting back to where they once belonged.  To have this music finally presented as it was originally intended -- sonically pristine and stripped of artifice or studio tinkering -- is a joy.    The remastering is amazing, allowing us to hear with clarity and detail just what the band was doing in the studio and on the rooftop; those who claim it sounds too clean or sterile should keep in mind that the original recordings were so clean sounding that they were bordering on being anemic.  (John Lennon referred to it as \\"badly recorded sh**\\", after all.) The reordering of the tracks also helps make this album seem more whole, more of a piece with itself.  Setting aside preexisting notions of how this album should be ordered based on the original \\"Let It Be\\" album, it really makes sense to start this album off with \\"Get Back\\" and to finish with \\"Let It Be.\\"  And while many feel that the removal of the chatter and banter makes the album less charming and \\"human,\\" it should be remembered that no original Beatles album had any such studio excerpts; every album that the band approved while they were together was a produced collection of songs, which is what we finally have with this material.  Having it available in this context finally gives closure to a musical story that, until now, felt incomplete.  Obviously, fans are split on how much they like this release.  Many people are complaining that this album is woefully lacking in completeness, that there is so much more material from these sessions that is going unreleased.  It is apparent, though, that completeness is not the purpose of this release.  \\"Naked\\" simply attempts to put the original \\"Let It Be\\" material into the form of an album The Beatles themselves, along with George Martin, would have assembled, rather than leaving the task to a heavy-handed third party.  In this regard, it succeeds brilliantly.  For those of us who always felt that \\"Let It Be\\" was a flawed gem in an otherwise flawless setting, \\"Let It Be...Naked\\" is a welcome, and much needed, addition to the Beatles catalogue.  I, for one, am glad that it is finally here.  The bonus disc is interesting, but not essential.  Fortunately, one is not required to pay any extra for it.  I think it was included as a salve to those who would rail against the removal of the chatter and banter from the actual album order.  However, it also helps tell the story of the evolution of the Get Back/Let It Be project.  One very interesting inclusion is the sound of John tinkering around on the piano with a sketchy instrumental arrangement of \\"Imagine.\\"

B0000DJZA5|5.0|The jacket of the original Let It Be album claimed that it had the \\"warmth & freshness of a live performance.\\"  That turned out to be a lie, thanks to one Phil Spector.  Nearly 34 years later, Paul, Ringo, & George (who gave this project his approval before his death) hope to correct what they and many others feel was an injustice.  The result is a dazzling feast for the ears...in other words, what the original album SHOULD have been--a true Beatles album.  There is no clash of live intimacy with overproduction.  Any trace of Spector's involvement is gone.  And good riddance.  The sound quality of Let It Be...Naked is so crisp (thanks in part to today's technology), each individual instrument, every voice can be heard so clearly, as if the Beatles are playing in your living room.  That's the first thing you'll notice upon hearing \\"Get Back,\\" which more sensefully opens the album this time.  The songs which sound the most different are \\"The Long And Winding Road\\" & \\"Across The Universe.\\"  If you've seen the Let It Be movie, you know how intimate \\"The Long And Winding Road\\" was meant to sound--as if Paul is playing piano and singing directly to you.  Gone are Spector's choir and extra instrumentation that Paul hated so much.  This song alone is truely worth the price of the album.  \\"Across The Universe,\\" in all its original simplicity, sounds almost like a demo.  It's very similar to the \\"wildlife\\" version but without the distracting sound effects and fades out in a ghostly echo.  \\"Let It Be\\" also sounds fresh & new, with Billy Preston's organ pushed way more towards the front of the mix.  My only disappointment is that the guitar solo is more similar to the unfortunately better-known single version rather than the ripping version overdubbed onto the original album version.  That's okay, though...it fits the mood of the album better this way.  The biggest surprises on this album come from the songs \\"One After 909\\" & \\"I've Got A Feeling,\\" two songs I never really careda whole lot for.  Before hearing Let It Be...Naked, I never realized how dreadfully muddy these songs sounded on the original album.  You can actually hear the keyboard in \\"I've Got A Feeling\\" now and the song sounds great.  Both songs are so much more coherent.  The only song that I felt benefited from Spector's production was \\"I Me Mine.\\"  It sounds a bit loose here (only three Beatles played on it, after all).  One of the rawest songs the Beatles ever recorded together, \\"Don't Let Me Down,\\" is presented here in all its glory, sounding more natural than its somewhat polished original.  The rest of the songs are ones which Spector did not impose on very much, so they don't sound too different from their versions on the original album.  However, the aforementioned clarity of sound is what makes these songs worth hearing again.  Some people may complain that \\"Maggie Mae\\" & \\"Dig It\\" are not included.  Well, \\"Maggie Mae\\" was always just an expendable jam (which they did many of during these sessions) and the short excerpt of the original jam \\"Dig It\\" on the original album is pretty much it.  The bonus disc called \\"Fly On The Wall\\" is somewhat interesting.  It's a mish-mash of the Beatles discussing their music & the direction of the original project and excerpts from a few unreleased songs & early takes.  The disc is a perfect supplement piece to the movie, but I doubt that anyone who's lucky enough to own the complete session bootlegs would find it interesting.  A lot more could have been included on this disc, but it's free so it's hard to complain.  My guess is that people like myself who are artists or interested in music production will get the most out of Let It Be...Naked. B000002C1V|5.0|This record saddens me.<br />Well, I guess the record doesn't sadden me nearly as much as the personal drama involved in its making, and fans' reaction (or overreaction) to that drama.<br />Yes, the band wasn't getting along at the time.  Yes, Roger Waters was attempting (and succeeding) to iron-fist the project.  No, the album is not a failure in any sense of the word.  Artistically, I would go so far as to say that this is arguably the single finest 'Floyd album ever produced.  That statement certainly depends a great deal on one's own personal tastes (I also like Meddle and The Wall), but since this is my review, my tastes are the ones that currently matter.<br />This record embodies Roger Waters' finest hour; his vocals are achingly emotional and drive the project flawlessly.  The Final Cut is certainly the most powerful record 'Floyd has ever produced.  Lyrically, no punches are pulled, and whether or not one agrees with the political statement made, one cannot doubt the sincerity of the message or the power of the delivery.<br />The arrangement and flow is superior, easily matching, if not surpassing, The Wall.  This record truly demonstrates Waters' genius, even when embroiled in the mess that was Pink Floyd at the time.  The mediocrity of 'Floyd's next record, A Momentary Lapse of Reason, serves to amplify and highlight the lyrical genius and overwhelming vision Roger Waters brought to the troubled table of this legendary band.<br />Judge this record on its artistic merits as opposed to the troubles of the band that produced it.

B0000DJZA5|4.0|Repeated listening to this record (sans rose-tinted memories) reveals a band working, playing and enjoying making music. The production is simple, clean and very friendly to the notion of a few blokes sitting in the basement playing and singing together. When you hear this, everything about the music that's not the Music just goes away. All the tired mythology, Onophobia, addictions, feuds, jealousies etc. having nothing to do with the strength of song and arrangement, evaporate. Strange, but this new record reminds me of the Grateful Dead - without the Spectorish orchestral mud, you can see these guys getting free and expressing themselves intuitively with whatever they want, the musics they love, a little country, a little blues, rockabilly. The freewheeling, music-genre-bending '60s San Francisco scene, rock-and-roll bands in cowboy hats, may have struck a few Beatles as a liberating relief compared to their usual workaholic studio sessioning. Naked's fundamentalist recording style plays up the voices most importantly: this is a singing group. Every lyric and syllable is clear and loud. The lads sing with great gusto, delivering the songs with conviction. I think this is a look at how the Beatles heard themselves as they sat around together in rehearsal and hacked out songs. That's a special treat. A couple of things on the Fly on the Wall disk are incredible: song snippets, &quot;All Things Must Pass&quot; played by the Beatles, Paul's lobbying his band to hang together and work on their career, John's dissipating non-sequiturs, the stupid idea about a concert on an ocean liner, the band falling apart before your ears...But it was a band, and this record is bandy as hell, all rocking, all present, all conscious, all Beatles in every note.

He is named Buddy and raised as an elf.  <br />The main story in the film is Buddy's search for his birthfamily.  Unfortunately, as you may guess, the film is full of references to the elf's \\"real father\\" i.e. his birth father. <br />Other minor irritations (from my perspective as an adoptive parent) include the fact that the elf's adoption was kept a \\"secret\\" from him until he was an adult; he is initally rejected by his birth father; and there is a subtle implication that one cannot be raised by a parent that is not of a child's birth culture.<br />On the positive side, Buddy the elf does find he has been enriched by his Elf family.  He doesn't seem to be mortified by his adoption (as in Country Bear) and on a few occasions he states with a bit of pride that he was adopted.  <br />The difficulty for me is that if the writers of this film had bothered to have a conversation with a few professionals in the adoption field, they could have made the movie less offensive for adoptive families.  This could have been a really fun movie even for families affected by adoption.  For example, the substitution of the loving title birthfather, rather than \\"real father\\".  Or, perhaps Buddy could have known along that he was adopted -- the scenes of an elf in Manhatten would have been just as funny.  <br />Just a cautionary note for adoptive families.

B0000DJZA5|5.0|This is the Way 'Let It Be' should have sounded when it was first released. Although I am confident that if the Beatles were able to hear this CD as released, they would have nitpicks that annoyed them about it, I am also confident that this is far closer to their original conception of 'Let It Be' than we have ever heard before. Although the fillers \\"Maggie Mae\\" and \\"Dig It\\" are notably (and I think, thankfully) absent, as is the banter from the rooftop, I think this is a much more pure album in concept, and certainly the digital processing has cleaned it up a lot. Gone are all the traces of \\"Spectorization\\" the guys (especially Paul) hated. Although never one of my favorites \\"The Long And Winding Road\\" sounds especially better without the extras.   Standouts for me are \\"Get Back\\" (although I do kind of miss the rooftop chatter), \\"One After 909\\", which sounds wonderful: it is now so much more polished and crisp than ever before, \\"I Me Mine\\", on which George and the guitar work sound especially powerful and the title track, \\"Let It Be\\", which is, evidently, a combination of edits, featuring the George solo from the movie, which I have always preferred above all the other solos (especially the John solo on the original LP) which sounds incredibly sharp. Ringo's drum part seems more pronounced and crisp, and the whole song comes across as the melodic masterpiece that it is.   Downsides for me are \\"I've Got a Feeling,\\" which, though never a personal favorite, is particularly raw to an uncomfortable degree, especially vocally, and the bonus CD \\"Fly On The Wall,\\" which could have been a good 'alternate takes' or 'deleted tracks' CD. Instead it is really a very disjointed set of snippets of different songs some of which you have heard before, and some you probably haven't (included are some songs that would shortly become solo releases of former Beatles, notably \\"All Things Must Pass\\"). The bonus is worth listening to about one time for a historical perspective, but in myjudgment that's about it.   There are things that long time Beatles fans such as myself would change about this CD, but overall it is indispensable and deserves to be on every music lover's CD rack. Buy it today!

0061015725|3.0|In Michael Crichton's latest novel, Prey, the author revisits one of his oldest themes, in particular the dangers and unintended consequences of developing new technologies.  In this case, the technological development is nanotechnology and, as typical with Crichton, the effort is a mixed success.<br />The story in this case involves a company that has developed miniature robots for medical diagnostics.  Necessary to their success is a program that allows them to act as a swarm (since any individual robot is too small to perform complex tasks); this swarm eventually gets loose from the supposedly secure facility and starts multiplying and preying on the local animals.  The narrator is called in to try and rein in the nanobots, but they are evolving faster than he can come up with ways to stop them.<br />Crichton is an idea guy, and this story - like many of his novels - is dominated by the idea at the cost of other considerations such as plot or character.  In fact, most of the characters are little more than talking heads designed to represent various viewpoints (the voice of reason, the amoral entrepreneur, etc.) in the discussion of the dangers of this nanotechnology.  Despite a string of best-selling novels, the number of memorable Crichton characters can be counted easily on one hand, even if you lost a couple fingers in an accident.<br />This story is not completely original, revisiting elements from such books as Jurassic Park, The Andromeda Strain and Sphere.  In the end, however, this is standard Crichton:  fast reading and thought provoking without really being good writing.

B0000TB05A|5.0|When I heard that this show was going to be released as a DVD, I was a little apprehensive. There was already a DVD issued from the Elevation tour and feared fans might find a home town event too parochial.<br />So many events conspired to make this show truly unique. Slane Castle has been host to Bob Dylan, Bruce Springsteen, Neil Young, Guns n' Roses, Thin Lizzy, Queen, Rolling Stones, Red Hot Chili Peppers (this year, and whose slane DVD is due 1 week after this one) but was never headlined by U2 until 2001.<br />The first Slane show on August 25th sold out in 10 minutes, 80000 tickets in 10 minutes. The result was that a second show had to be held. This meant that planning permission had to be pushed through very quickly by the Irish government.<BR> <BR>The setting for the concert is unique and magnificent. The hill outside the castle is a natural amphitheatre. The castle itself was lit up with various colours throughout the show. Behind the castle lies the river Boyne, the setting for the 1690 battle that has left so much emotional baggage between the 2 people who share the island of Ireland.<br />The 2 other events that make this show equally jubilant and emotional is fact the Bono was in mourning over the death of his father. The funeral was the eve of the first Slane show. However, the truly magnificent event that had the crowd on a high all day was the World cup qualifier held that afternoon in Dublin. The 2nd half of the match was screened to the Slane audience.<BR> <BR>A chance of an Irish victory was slim and was further reduced when Ireland where reduced to 10 men. Imagine the ecstasy of the crowd when Jason Mc Ateer hammered home the only goal of the game to knock the mighty Dutch out of the world cup and keep Ireland on the path to the world cup.<BR> <BR>These 2 events are so apparent during a lot of the banter with the crowd. 'Kite' is dedicated to his father Bob Hewson, Bono changes the words of beautiful day to 'beautiful goal' in reference to Jason Mc Ateer's wonder goal. (He did dedicate 'One' to Jason Mc Ateer, but this was edited out for one reason or another, most obvious one being the scope of Mc Ateer's fame, the other may have been an attempt to ease the suffering of Dutch U2 fans).<BR> <BR>Other highlights include, Sunday Bloody Sunday, in which Bono recalls the names of the victims of the 1998 Omagh bombing. This is quite powerful as it helps to humanise the victims instead of making them statistics of a bloody outrage. The Boyne river in the background makes the message even more potent and relevant.<BR>Bullet the blue sky is brilliant. The introduction by Sinead O Connor is inspired. However they did leave out the Charlton Heston footage at the end of the song. The only cringe-worthy shot is Bono's Ireland flag waving moment, however considering the days events, he does go out of his way to explain its 'just once, just once for Jason Mc Ateer' and not some jingoistic retarded nationalistic jesture. 'Mysterious ways' was taken from the main track selection and placed on the DVD as a bonus track. No idea why, but its a great tune made all the better by Bono's daughter accompanying him on stage. Likewise he dedicates 'All I want is you' to his wife Ali. Bono also summons up their early days with a rendition of their first single 'Out of control'. During which he recounts the story of raising the 2000 pounds necessary to fund the band and how the band decided to stay and live in Dublin with 'the tribe'.<br />This is a truly unique, excellent and uplifting DVD that all U2 fans will enjoy and which U2 themselves will find difficult to replicate again.

B00005B36H|4.0|When I picked this disc up, I did so with a sort of subconscience resistance. Overtly speaking, I was not exactly a Tool fan and had heard enogh God- bashing Maynard James Keenan sound bytes to frighten a small army of nuns. But, I gave fate a chance, and emerged upon the exit of this listening experience, a happier and changed man. Of course, I'm kidding. Despair breeds despair, and there are hints of such tension on the tracks. But, and let me say BUT, I actually found a sense of refreshment in Maynard's lyrics that alluded to forgivness, self-acceptance and a healthily balanced momentum toward postive change. This isn't \\"We are the world\\", but it is the least sharply offending Tool disc I have ever heard. The songs, pretty much , all roll along as mini-opus's that are sprawlingly evacotive  , but with tighly wound cohesion and formulaic warmth. The passion is here and on songs like \\"The Patient\\", \\"Schism\\"(the record's best track)and \\"The Grudge\\", the door is open to healing and transformation. These are good themes, and not exactly excpected ones from Tool's main vocalist and lyrist.The only break-out/freak-outs of fury, occur on \\"Ticks and Leeches\\" and although it weirdly sounds homosexually oriented, it isn't;instead it's about being taken advantage of, whether by thier overwhelming popularity, artistic temperment, or personal lives.The song doesn't offend, but it breaks up the running pace of thematiclly weaved issues of forgivness and communication. In closing, I can't say that this is a dark-free, happy go lucky record, but I can say that it's a nice change, not only in Tool's already evolving musical sound, but also in the lyrics; ones that actually point upward to healing instead of down to despair and continued frustration. I'm not exactly a Tool fan, but this disc is very good(although there are some filler tracks, mostly lame instrumentals) and I can rightly reccomend it. \\"The Grudge\\" and \\"Schism\\" are the record's best tracks and I personally think that\\"Schism\\" is one of the best modern rock songs of all time. As a Christian, I don't exactly find myself drawn to darkly disturbing peices of art, but this one is a rare oddity that points to hope's shining star, and it is a big crowning achievment for Tool, one of which I hope continues. <BR>Cheers!

Said truth be told, IT in fact IS an excellent album, one of the supreme best of it's kind, emerging with spectacular production values, innovative musical skill, and bold, defiant songwriting, from Serj's vocal prowess, to the underpinning radical social commentary. Unfortunatly, I am not in the \\"System's \\" majority who feel that the government is a turncoat threat to the welfare of our country, or in regard, to the prospering policy of other nations. \\"Prison Song\\" is an excellent song, complete with mid-tempo changes, witty lyrics(Malakian is a toungue in cheek prodigy) and style to spare, but frankly, our governement is not the epitome of an evil empire. System rails against the system and that is some what of a turn-off, because as a proud american and Christian who stands next to Bush, I can't support lyrics that scream anarchistic defiance toward towering authority figures. A divided nation cannot stand(thank you Jesus and Washington) and their political views slightly obscure and mar my listening appreciation for the band. Anyway, politics aside, the disc is musically excellent. The only cuts that are just so-so involve \\"Bounce\\", \\"Shimmy\\" and \\"Science\\". The rest of the disc stacks up as a mammoth plethora of meaningful ferocity. So, while I can't condone the philosophy that the band has sharply imbued, I can say that this music alone is some of the best hard rock music ever written. As a Christian, I don't find myself listening to the album any more (it's long since been buried somewhere upstairs in one of my filing cabinets)because I object to the tarty sexual innuendo of the crass and musically underpolished \\"Bounce\\" and while \\"Chop Suey!\\" may be the most powerfully great song of extremes ever written, I've learned that God forsakes no one if they turn to His son. The 5 star review is given because it is a stellar musical recording. Unfortunatly, they havn't yet invented a scrambler to change the lyrics.
